The Carey School’s Parking and Traffic Plan  
This plan has been updated to include the proposed Carey School Early Childhood Center 
(ECC) on La Salle Drive into Carey’s existing Parking and Traffic Plan. This plan, including on 
designated pick-up and drop-off locations, traffic flow, and escort requirements, is shared with 
the Carey Community each year and reminders are sent regularly to require all community 
members to follow the plan as outlined below. The requirements of this plan will be monitored 
by Carey’s Head of School, Director of Operations and Director of Facilities. 
One of our primary responsibilities as neighborhood community members is to execute a safe 
and efficient parking and traffic plan. The plan that follows is one we have developed in 
collaboration with the City of San Mateo and our neighbors. The principal goals of the plan are 
two: 
1.​ To ensure the safety of the children, the school community, and those in the 
neighborhood. 
2.​ To create an efficient flow of traffic to minimize the traffic impact on the neighborhood 
for daily school operations and special events. 
We appreciate all you do to maintain good relationships with our neighbors, and therefore, we 
require your full adherence to this plan at all times. 
A. Staggered Start and End Times 
The school has early morning care beginning at 7:45 am and extended care each afternoon until 
6:00 pm. We ask that you utilize the chart below for the location and drop-off and pick-up 
times of each grade:

*Note: If you have more than one child in the school, please use the designated area/time for

B. Staffing 
●​
During peak morning times, there will be five administrators at drop-off and pick-up 
areas (on the Alameda and on the La Salle circle), and one staff member on the corner 
of 20th Ave and La Salle (denoted by the numeral 5) from 8:00-8:25 am and 3:00-3:30 
pm to assist children and to help direct the flow of traffic. Please see the exhibits of the 
La Salle circle below where the numbers denote administrator on duty.  
●​
During peak afternoon times, each lead and assistant teacher for grades will supervise 
the pick-up for the respective grades. 
●​
All school staff on duty use walkie talkies to communicate and create a safe and efficient 
process.  
Note: Additionally, the administrators on duty will help ensure that the driveways are free for 
neighbor use (driveways denoted on exhibit with X). On rare occasions, when a car in the 
queue blocks the flow of traffic, they will be asked to drive around the block.  
C. Preschool Student Logistics 
*The following logistics apply to all locations preschool students are dropped off and picked up 
(La Salle, Alameda, ECC parking lot) 
●​
Preschool students with siblings should be dropped off and picked up with their oldest 
sibling 
●​
To ensure safe exit and entrance of cars on La Salle and Alameda, preschool students 
should be seated closest to the rear passenger door so staff can ensure their safe exit 
and entrance to vehicles. 
●​
School staff shall be assigned to escort all preschool students from all designated pick-up 
locations on La Salle and the Alameda to the school building, and from the building back 
to each pick-up location. 
One Carey School Lane • San Mateo, CA 94403 • Phone (650) 345-8205 • Fax (650) 345-2528 • www.careyschool.org

--- page 151 ---
●​
While assigned staff are escorting students to or from the building, another staff 
member shall remain stationed at each drop-off and pick-up location to receive arriving 
preschool students and ensure continuous supervision. Each location must always have 
at least one dedicated staff member present during the entire pick-up and drop-off 
period. 
●​
Students shall be escorted by school staff or parents/guardians between the 2033 La 
Salle parking lot and the school building. Unaccompanied students are not permitted to 
walk between these areas. 
D. Traffic Procedures La Salle (Grades PS, K, 1, 4) 
All vehicles are required to drive slowly on La Salle, when turning off of and returning to 20th 
Ave.   
 
*Numbers in exhibit are personnel locations 
Rules of the Road: 
One Carey School Lane • San Mateo, CA 94403 • Phone (650) 345-8205 • Fax (650) 345-2528 • www.careyschool.org

--- page 152 ---
●​
Please do not park on the La Salle Circle during peak hours as it is used for drop-off and 
pick-up. 
●​
Pull all the way forward on La Salle by completing the full circle around the cul-de-sac.  
The forward-most drop-off and pick-up location would be just beyond the school’s 
driveway. 
●​
In order to ensure safe access and egress to and from the neighborhood, please stay in 
your vehicle at all times and follow the flow of traffic and do not leave the line to turn 
back around, especially by using a neighbor’s driveway. 
●​
Staff will direct cars into each designated drop-off/pick-up spot in the circle and 
supervise children entering/exiting the vehicle. 
●​
Families and faculty/staff are asked not to park on La Salle during the school day. If you 
need to come to campus, utilize parking on the Alameda, in the ECC parking lot, 2000 
Alameda or short-term parking in the circle outside of carline operation.  
E. Traffic Procedures Alameda (Grades 2, 3, 5) 
All vehicles are required to drive slowly on the Alameda into the white zone. The School 
maintains a speed limit monitor to encourage drivers to maintain a safe speed.  Be advised that 
parking will not be allowed from the end of the white zone to the end of the red zone fire 
hydrant on the Alameda from 2:30 pm – 3:30 pm. 
 
*Numbers in exhibit are personnel locations 
Rules of the Road: 
One Carey School Lane • San Mateo, CA 94403 • Phone (650) 345-8205 • Fax (650) 345-2528 • www.careyschool.org

--- page 153 ---
●​
The white zone in front of the school is designated as a drop-off/pick-up zone only.  
Please do not park in this area. 
●​
In order to ensure the safety of passenger cars behind you, please stay in your vehicle at 
all times and pull all the way forward on the Alameda to the forward-most spot in the 
white zone (Note that this spot is several car lengths in front of the front gate). 
●​
Staff will direct cars into each designated drop-off/pick-up spot in the white zone and 
supervise children entering/exiting the vehicle. 
F. Early Childhood Center Drop-Off and Pick-Up 
 
*Numbers in exhibit are personnel locations 
 
Rules of the Road: 
●​
Pull all the way forward on La Salle, complete the full circle and then pull into the ECC 
driveway.  Pull as far forward as possible in the drop-off lane to keep traffic flowing.  
One Carey School Lane • San Mateo, CA 94403 • Phone (650) 345-8205 • Fax (650) 345-2528 • www.careyschool.org

--- page 154 ---
●​
Staff will direct cars into each designated drop-off/pick-up spot in the parking lot and 
supervise children entering/exiting the vehicle. Remain in your car unless directed 
otherwise by staff.  
●​
In order to ensure safe access and egress to and from the neighborhood, please follow 
the one-way loop from W 20th Ave down La Salle Dr through the drop-off loop and do 
not jump the line, follow the flow of traffic. If dropping off, have your child signed in on 
Procare and have their backpacks and belongings ready to speed up unloading. 
F. Extended Care Drop-Off and Pick-Up During Non-Peak Times 
School is open as of 7:45 am and those dropping off before 8:00 am must walk students to the 
gate where they are monitored upon entry to the premises. The Alameda (non-white zone) and 
La Salle cul-de-sac are available for parking during these times. When picking up your student 
from extended care, please park parallel on Alameda or in the ECC parking lot.  The faculty 
parking lot is not available for parent parking unless otherwise specified (see Special Events). 
G. Special Events 
The School has established parking routines for special events are tailored to the anticipated 
parking need of the particular event in an effort to minimize the impact on the neighborhood.  
For nighttime events, the campus grounds may be used for parking, which increases the onsite 
spaces by 30.  The school partners with the commercial property at 2000 Alameda to handle 
overflow parking or for parents that wish to park and walk their children into school.  
H. Contacting the School 
In the event you have observations or concerns about the parking or traffic conditions near The 
Carey School, please contact Head of School, Neely Norris at (650) 345-8205 or send an email 
to nnorris@careyschool.org.   
I. Help the Environment 
To relieve traffic and to help the environment, we encourage the following modes of 
transportation to and from school: 
●​ Walk/Bike to School: The Carey School Bi-Ped Club - Contact The Carey School for 
more information. 
 
●​ Carpool to School: School Pools - The School maintains a GoKid account which is a 
convenient and reliable school carpooling program for schools which helps families form 
One Carey School Lane • San Mateo, CA 94403 • Phone (650) 345-8205 • Fax (650) 345-2528 • www.careyschool.org

--- page 155 ---
and manage carpools. ​
 
Also, when in the queue, turn your car off if you will be waiting for more than a minute.  Idling 
wastes gas and causes more pollution than restarting your car. 
Thank you for your assistance in making The Carey School safe and for being a 
responsible community member. 
If you have any questions about these protocols, please contact Neely Norris, Head of School at 
nnorris@careyschool.org or (650)345-2044. 
One Carey School Lane • San Mateo, CA 94403 • Phone (650) 345-8205 • Fax (650) 345-2528 • www.careyschool.org

WHEREAS, Licensee desires to receive a license to use certain parking spaces in the
surface parking lot 1900, 1950, 2000 Alameda de las Pulgas - Exterior Parking Lot ("Parking

be located exclusively within the area of the Parking Lot as shown on Exhibit A attached

utilize the Parking Lot or any or all of the Spaces. Licensee shall not have the right to
use more than the number of Spaces set forth herein. Licensee shall provide Licensor
with a list of the license plate numbers for all vehicles authorized to park on the
Leased Premises not less than monthly.

2. The term of this Agreement shall be for 1 month commencing on August 1, 2018 (the
“Gommencement Date’) and ending on July 31, 2019, provided that the term of this
Agreement shall automatically be extended thereafter on a month-to-month basis until
terminated by either Licensor or Licensee by the giving of at least 30 days written notice
of termination to the other party.

3. On or before the first day of each month during the term of this A reement, Licensee
shall pay to Licensor the sum of Seven Hundred and Fifty Dollars INI er month,

company (“Licensor”) and THE CAREY SCHOOL, a California non-profit corporation (“Licensee”)

“Original License Agreement”) pursuant to which Licensor grants to Licensee, on a month-to-month
basis, a non-exclusive license to use certain “Spaces” in the surface “Parking Lot” which is currently
controlled by Licensor and commonly referred to as 1950 and 2000 Alameda de las Pulgas, San Mateo,
California, WHERAS all capitalized terms used in this Amendment but not otherwise defined shall have
the meanings assigned to them in the Original License;

B. WHEREAS, Licensee and Licensor desire to modify the terms of the Original License
Agreement; and whereas the Original License Agreement, as modified by this Amendment, shall be
referred to as the “Agreement”;

contained herein, and for other good and valuable consideration, the receipt and sufficiency of which are
hereby acknowledged, the parties hereto agree to amend the Original Lease as follows:

4. Counterparts. This Amendment may be executed in counterparts, including electronic
or “DocuSign” counterparts, each of which shall be deemed an original and both of which together shall

to as 1950 and 2000 Alameda de las Pulgas, San Mateo, California; and WHERAS all capitalized terms 
used in this Amendment but not otherwise defined shall have the meanings assigned to them in the 
Existing Agreement;

WHEREAS, Licensor and Licensee desire to modify the terms and conditions set forth in 
the Second Amendment with respect to the number of spaces permitted for Special Event(s); and whereas

contained herein, and for other good and valuable consideration, the receipt and sufficiency of which are

use of the Spaces shall include, without any additional charge or fee, Licensee’s right to use up to 150 
Spaces to accommodate overflow parking for Licensee’s guests and invitees (including parents) for and 
during certain special events hosted by Licensee from time to time outside of normal business hours, on 
weekends, on holidays, or as otherwise approved by Licensor (“Special Event(s)”).

or “DocuSign” counterparts, each of which shall be deemed an original and both of which together shall 
constitute one and the same agreement.   
3.

the Existing Agreement shall remain in full force and effect.  In the event of a conflict between the terms 
of the Existing Agreement and the terms of this Amendment, the terms in this Amendment shall control.

Memorandum 
 
October 29, 2025 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Project # 4907-01 
 
To:  
Michelle Huber, StudioBondy Architecture; Mara Chase, Carey School  
 
 
 
From:  
Kelly Hardwicke, H. T. Harvey & Associates  
 
 
Subject:  
Riparian Setback Assessment for Early Childhood Center for Carey Schoool 
Project, City of San Mateo, San Mateo County, California  
 
 
 
Executive Summary. This memorandum was prepared to document the appropriate riparian setback for a 
construction of a new Early Childhood Center for Carey School at 1 Carey School Lane in the City of San Mateo. 
The Early Childhood Center (project) would be located at an adjacent parcel, at 2033 LaSalle Drive, Assessor’s 
Parcel Number (APN)-039040080 (Figure 1). An unnamed creek serves as the property line between the 2033 
LaSalle Drive project parcel and Carey School. This memorandum summarizes the habitat characteristics of the 
unnamed creek and riparian corridor located along the north edge of the creek, and provides our opinion of the 
appropriate, San Mateo-required riparian setback. The project parcel was evaluated by qualified biologists to 
determine the limits of sensitive riparian habitat within the unnamed creek, the TOB, and also to evaluate the 
potential for impacts to this habitat from the project. Using this data, setbacks appropriate to City codes were 
established (Figure 2), and new project buildings will entirely avoid these setbacks. No riparian trees will be 
removed by the project, and there will be no encroachment into riparian habitat beyond the existing development. 
The project is expected to have only minor, temporary, self restoring impacts to riparian habitat and no impacts 
to the creek itself.  
 
Regulatory Setting. The City of San Mateo has a city-wide setback requirement for waterways, Municipal Code 
7.39.130: Watercourse Protection (City of San Mateo 2024)1. Development setback from the unnamed creek was 
determined as described in Municipal Code 7.39.130 (a) and (b)(3). Additionally, our riparian setback assessment 
evaluated the riparian setback from the unnamed creek in accordance with the slope setback requirements 
applicable to stream banks described in City of San Mateo Slope Development Standards, pages 2-6 (City of San 
Mateo 1998).2 We have also mapped and discussed the minimal and less than significant potential impacts to 
 
1 City of San Mateo. 2024. City of San Mateo Municipal Code, City of San Mateo Law Library. Accessed at: 
https://law.cityofsanmateo.org/us/ca/cities/san-mateo/code in November 2024 
2 City of San Mateo. 1998. Slope Development Standards: Questions and Answers about Building on Sloped Property. 
Available from https://www.cityofsanmateo.org/DocumentCenter/View/92653/Slope-Development-Standards

--- page 168 ---
2 
H. T. Harvey & Associates 
sensitive riparian habitat associated with the unnamed stream, which are typically analyzed under CEQA 
Appendix G, Biological Resources Guideline B, which covers impacts to riparian habitat and other sensitive 
natural communities.  
 
Methods. H. T. Harvey & Associates plant and wetland ecologist Katherine Marlin, M.S., conducted a 
reconnaissance-level survey of the project site, defined as parcel 039040080 on October 30, 2024. The purpose 
of the survey was to provide creek and riparian habitat mapping, including top of bank and riparian dripline, and 
to measure various creek dimensions such as average depth and horizontal bank distance to apply the correct 
slope setback methods. We also mapped the location of top of bank (TOB), as well as the riparian dripline on 
the north bank of the creek, using sub-meter GPS. Creek depth was estimated using an inclinometer and verified 
using ESRI’s aggregated topographic LIDAR data for the area (ESRI, 2024).   Top of bank was mapped for the 
northern creek bank using methods from A Field Guide to the Identification of the Ordinary High Water Mark (OHWM) 
in the Arid West Region of the Western United States: A Delineation Manual (USACE 2008).3 
 
Within the riparian dripline and creek banks, we mapped sensitive riparian habitat. Riparian habitat was mapped 
based on the canopy line of trees rooted within TOB and changes in the vegetative community, from the 
understory species described in the preceding paragraph, to planted, ornamental species. Riparian habitat would 
also include all areas below the TOB. However, it should be noted that the areas underneath the riparian canopy 
that overhang TOB at this site were developed as yard and ornamental vegetation, and would be considered 
developed beyond TOB. Observations of the creek conditions and habitats were also noted. TOB was recorded 
by flagging the TOB in the field and then surveying this line in using accurate survey methods.  
 
City of San Mateo Municipal Code 7.39.130: WATERCOURSE PROTECTION applies to all watercourses, 
public and private, throughout the city (City of San Mateo 2024).  Therefore, our setback analysis focused on the 
WATERCOURSE PROTECTION code and the City’s Slope Development Standards, which apply to creek 
banks. 
 
In accordance with the City of San Mateo Municipal Code 7.39.130: WATERCOURSE PROTECTION, under 
Chapter 7.39: Stormwater Management and Discharge (City of San Mateo 2024):  
 
“(a) Every person owning property through which a watercourse passes, or such person's lessee or 
tenant, shall keep and maintain that part of the watercourse within the property reasonably free of 
trash, debris, excessive vegetation, and other obstacles which would pollute, contaminate, or 
significantly retard the flow of water through the watercourse; shall maintain existing privately owned 
structures within or adjacent to a watercourse, so that such structures will not become a hazard to the 
use, function, or physical integrity of the watercourse; and shall not remove healthy bank vegetation 
beyond that actually necessary for said maintenance, nor remove said vegetation in such a manner as to 
increase the vulnerability of the watercourse to erosion. 
 
 
 
3 [USACE] U.S. Army Corps of Engineers. 2008. A Field Guide to the Identification of the Ordinary High Water Mark 
(OHWM) in the Arid West Region of the Western United States: A Delineation Manual. ERDC/CRREL TR-08-12.

--- page 169 ---
3 
H. T. Harvey & Associates 
(b) No person shall commit or cause to be committed any of the following acts, unless a written permit 
has first been obtained from the Director of Public Works: 
 
… (3) Carry out development within 30 feet of the center line of any creek or 20 feet of the 
top of a bank; …or,  
 
(6) Place any loose or unconsolidated material along the side of or within a watercourse or so 
close to the side as to cause a diversion of the flow, or to cause a probability of such material 
being carried away by stormwaters passing through such watercourse.”  
 
 
City of San Mateo Slope Development Standards state that the slope setback distance is determined by a 
combination of slope steepness, slope height at top of slope, and/or a calculation of distance related to slope 
height and horizonal/vertical distance (City of San Mateo 1998). As per the Slope Development Standards:  
 
“for a slope over 25% up to and including 50%, and 10 feet to 30 feet height top of slope, the setback 
from top of slope is the greater of either: 
 
15 feet,  
 
The diagonal distance of 3:1, 3 horizontal to 1 vertical measured from the toe slope, or,  
 
A distance equal to the slope height from the top of slope.”  
 
In this case, we defined “toe slope” to be the bottom of the creek, which was approximated well by the current 
property line which is a stream centerline, and “top of slope” to be TOB.  
 
We chose three points along the creek which each represent approximately 1/3 of the length of the creek, each 
with unique characteristics including ground cover and slope percent. Then, using an inclinometer, we measured 
degree of slope steepness from the toe of slope. Horizontal distance from toe of slope to top of slope/bank was 
measured using GPS. Finally, the depth from toe of slope to the bottom of the creek was visually estimated  and 
trigonometric calculations, and verified using LIDAR topographic data (ESRI, 2024). 
 
Results. The approximately 0.6-acre project parcel is located in the midst of urban-suburban development, 3.5 
miles east of Crystal Springs and 4 miles west of the San Francisco Bay (Figure 1). The reach of the unnamed 
creek adjacent to the parcel is channelized and runs perpendicular to LaSalle Drive. The creek does not appear in 
maps from National Wetlands Inventory or U.S. Geological Survey, and is undergrounded in culverts and bridges 
both up- and down-stream of the project site. At the time of the October survey, there was low flow with about 
3-6 inches of water. Since October is towards the end of the dry season, and the conditions were normal at the 
time of the survey, this unnamed creek is likely perennial (EPA 2024).4 The creek channel is incised, likely due to 
high flows during previous rainy seasons’ storm events. There is a three-foot-wide culvert at the west end of the 
unnamed creek, a chain-link fence near the midway point with a handmade structure that allows for water flow, 
 
4 [EPA] Environmental Protection Agency. 2024. Antecedent Precipitation Tool [Software]. Available from https:// 
www.epa.gov/wotus/antecedent-precipitation-tool-apt.

--- page 170 ---
4 
H. T. Harvey & Associates 
and an opening at the east end which drains under the road. The eastern drainage is piped underground after the 
road crossing. The low-flow channel is approximately 3 feet wide and has a sandy substrate filled with 3-4 inch 
wide, gravelly rocks. The creek bottom is unvegetated, does not support wetlands, and tree roots stick out of the 
break in slope delimiting the low flow channel.  
 
The mapped TOB line and riparian canopy and habitat are shown on Figure 2. The riparian area within the project 
parcel was determined by identifying trees rooted below or right at the top of bank on the 2033 LaSalle Drive 
side of the creek, and whose canopies overhung the creek substantially. Riparian habitat did not include any trees 
rooted beyond top of bank, even if they had continuous canopy cover with trees rooted below top of bank, as 
many of these appeared planted and did not have any biological relation to the stream, being located well above 
the stream’s water table or expected area of normal flooding. The riparian area within the parcel has an 
approximately 50% slope (2:1). The slopes are more gradual at the east and west extremities, and steepest in the 
middle. Overstory tree species included some natives such as coast live oak (Quercus agrifolia) and cottonwood 
(Populus fremontii), as well as non-natives such as Japanese cheesewood (Pittosporum tobira), brush cherry (Syzygium 
paniculatum), silky oak (Grevillea robusta), and Peruvian pepper tree (Schinus molle). A planted coast redwood (Sequoia 
sempervirens) was rooted just above the edge of top of bank at the eastern reach and is not considered a part of the 
riparian drip line. This redwood is a part of a continuous row of landscaped yard trees that are planted in a flat, 
sandy landscaped area. Understory vegetation species included nightshade (Solanum sp.), smooth leaved elm 
(Ulmus minor), bitter lettuce (Lactuca virosa), French broom (Genista monspessulana), Himalayan blackberry (Rubus 
armeniacus), bull thistle (Cirsium vulgare), nettleleaf goosefoot (Chenopodium murale), periwinkle (Vinca major), English 
ivy (Hedera helix), and stream orchid (Epipactis sp.), all of which were vegetative at the time of the survey.  
 
Upland, developed habitat north of the riparian habitat was flat and entirely developed/landscaped, consisting of 
concrete driveway, ornamental species such as aloe (Aloe sp.), or jade (Crassula sp.), turf grasses, and redwoods 
along the east edge of the parcel. Developed plantings occurred under the riparian dripline beyond TOB and 
would not be considered sensitive habitat at the ground level, even though the riparian canopy overhangs this 
area. Photos of the creek channel, riparian area, and adjacent upland are presented in Appendix B. 
 
Stream/Slope Setback. Slope height was 10, 8.5, and 7 feet at data points 1, 2 and 3 respectively. The average 
vertical slope height at top of slope/bank was 8.5 feet. Therefore, the minimum slope setback for new buildings, 
as described in the Slope Development Standards, is 15 feet from TOB. This is greater than either the 3:1 distance 
from toe of slope or the distance of the depth from TOB.   
 
The Watercourse Setback was evaluated as 20 feet from TOB, which was greater than 30 feet from toe of slope. 
Figure 2 in Appendix A shows the results from the survey on October 30, 2024, including TOB, stream 
centerline/toe of slope, dripline for the north bank riparian vegetation, and the slope and watercourse setbacks. 
New buildings proposed by the project avoid both setbacks.  
 
Riparian Impacts under CEQA. The proposed project associated with this riparian setback survey is 
anticipated to have less than significant impacts on the riparian area and no impacts on the creek itself. The

--- page 171 ---
5 
H. T. Harvey & Associates 
proposed project will not be removing any riparian trees or trees within the riparian corridor, and has been 
designed to avoid all impacts to the creek, creek banks, and riparian corridor. Tree protection zones will be 
established around all trees to be preserved by the project, with arborist monitoring for work that must occur 
within these zones. Project activities are planned as replacement-in-kind within the existing developed footprint. 
However, any development that may occur under the riparian canopy that overhangs the site will only affect the 
existing developed habitat, and will not introduce new buildings within this area. Within the developed area under 
the overhanging riparian canopy and adjacent to this area within the setback, existing landscaping will be updated 
for landscaping consistent with the requirements of the Early Childhood Center, such as garden nooks, a 
vegetable garden and sand and mud play areas. There would be no new hardscape under the riparian canopy and 
no increase in hardscape within the setback area from existing conditions.  
 
Implementation of the proposed project could result in temporary impacts to the adjacent mixed riparian 
woodland habitat (i.e., trimming of riparian trees or minor impacts to roots) as a result of construction in the 
landscaped urban-suburban strip along the riparian canopy edge (Figure 2), however these impacts are expected 
to be minor, would restore naturally after construction, and would not constitute a permanent reduction in 
riparian canopy. During construction, the riparian trees and the sensitive areas below TOB would be protected 
with appropriate Best Management Practices (BMPs), which may include fiber rolls and Environmentally 
Sensitive Area fencing. The project would not be expected to introduce additional human presence within the 
riparian habitat, as there are no plans for the Center to utilize, develop, or in any other way permanently impact 
the riparian habitat or creek. Given the existing levels of disturbance, the replacement of existing landscaping 
with new landscaping (thus not increasing hardscape within the developed area under the riparian canopy), the 
implementation of BMPs that will be utilized by the project to protect the sensitive riparian habitat and the stream, 
and the lack of any permanent disturbance to any sensitive riparian habitat or areas below TOB, and the full 
avoidance of both applicable City setbacks for the new building, the impacts to riparian habitats from the project 
would be considered less than significant.

Hi Patrick, 
  
Thank you for reaching out. Please note that the project has been recently submitted to the city, and staff 
is still conducting the first round of review. The information provided below is based on the initial 
application submitted by the applicant and may be updated as we receive additional information and 
materials in subsequent submittals. 
  
Please see my responses below in blue: 
  
1. Can you summarize this process?  The request is for a Special Use Permit and a Site Plan and 
Architectural Review to demolish the existing single-family residence and accessory dwelling unit 
(ADU) on site and construct a new early education center in a residential zoning district. The 
criteria and review process for the requested permits includes an application completeness 
review and an evaluation of compliance with the Zoning Code, General Plan, and state law 
requirements. Additionally, the project will be reviewed in compliance with the California 
Environmental Quality Act (CEQA). Staff will review the application materials based on these 
requirements and provide comments to the applicant. Once all comments have been addressed, 
the project will be scheduled for a public hearing with prior public notification.  
  
2. Is this process documented online? Yes, the application materials and the latest updates will be 
uploaded to the "What’s Happening" page for the project. Please visit this link for the most up-to-
date information. We will continue to update the site as we receive new information from the

--- page 183 ---
2
applicant. Additionally, I will include you on our interested party list to send you notifications 
about future public hearings related to this project. 
  
3. What makes criteria like an environmental study or a traffic impact study necessary? Typically, 
project potential impacts trigger environmental studies. In this case, the project will be required 
to provide several technical studies, such as traffic, noise, and parking assessments, to 
determine the appropriate level of environmental review in accordance with CEQA.  
  
4. How about laws such as SB 9 and statements from City Hall declaring they will do everything to 
preserve and increase the number of single-family residences in San Mateo. Does the planning 
department get involved in those issues? Yes, staff will evaluate the project in accordance with 
state housing laws. 
  
  
Comments are welcomed throughout the process. If you have additional questions, feel free to reach out 
to me. 
  
Thank you,

Petition Against the Carey School Expansion In Our Neighborhood 
San Mateo, CA 
 
To:  Building Planning Department of San Mateo, The Carey School Administration 
 
We, the undersigned residents of San Mateo, are writing to express our strong 
opposition to the proposed expansion of The Carey School in our neighborhood.  While 
we respect the right of the school to grow and improve, we believe that the current 
expansion plans will have serious negative consequences for our community. 
 
We oppose the expansion for the following reasons: 
1.​
Increased Traffic and Congestion 
The proposed expansion will undoubtedly lead to a significant increase in traffic around 
our neighborhood, causing congestion and making our streets less safe.  We are 
already experiencing challenges with the current traffic, particularly during school 
drop-off and pick-up times.  The Carey School has already placed “No Parking” signs in 
the curve of La Salle Street, noting there is “No Parking” during 8:00-8:30 a.m. and 
2:30-3:30 p.m.  These signs are seemingly illegal as there is no enforcing municipal 
code cited on the signs.  La Salle is a city street NOT the driveway to The Carey 
School.  The homeowners on this street pay taxes to support the roadways of the City 
of San Mateo, and should have an expectation that La Salle not be completely blocked 
for two hours each and every school day.  There is typically no way for emergency 
vehicles to access La Salle during the very congested drop-off and pick-up times.  Also, 
residents who are legally parked on La Salle during these times cannot move their 
vehicles off the street because they are blocked by cars lined up in the middle of the 
street waiting to pick up their students.  This expansion will exacerbate these issues, 
endangering the safety of children and pedestrians, as well as causing delays for 
residents. 
2.​
Environmental Impact 
The expansion will lead to increased construction and additional environmental strain, 
including noise, air pollution, and the reduction of some green space.  There are plans 
to eliminate 10 to 20 mature trees and it is unclear whether these trees have been 
inspected to see if they are considered protected heritage trees.  Our neighborhood’s 
natural beauty and quality of life would be compromised.  
3.​
Increased Noise Pollution 
An expanded school with more students and facilities will lead to an increase in noise 
pollution in the area.  This could negatively impact the well-being and mental health of 
residents, especially those living close to the school.  We value our quiet, residential 
environment, and believe the expansion would disrupt that peace.  Adding up to 75 
students playing outside, even closer to residences than the current set-up, will be 
problematic at best.

--- page 188 ---
4.​
Strain on Parking 
The streets surrounding The Carey School - 20th Avenue, Alameda de las Pulgas and 
La Salle - are already massively impacted with vehicles parking during school hours and 
after hour events.  Adding even more teachers, administrative staff, parents, and 
delivery trucks will make our residential neighborhood parking almost impossible.  There 
are many homeowners and renters at the abutting Gardens condominium complex who 
have more than one car and utilize the neighborhood for parking.  As homeowners, we 
again are paying taxes for the upkeep and typical use of these streets which should 
include a reasonable expectation of street parking. 
5.​
Loss of Residential Character 
Our neighborhood is primarily residential, and the expansion of The Carey School would 
change the character of the area, turning it into a more commercialized zone.  This 
would have a lasting impact on the sense of community and the desirability of our 
neighborhood for current and future residents. 
6.​
Property Value Concerns 
The proposed expansion could significantly decrease property values in the immediate 
vicinity due to increased traffic, noise, and disruption.  Many residents have invested in 
their homes with the understanding that the neighborhood would remain a quiet, 
residential area.  An expansion of this magnitude could undermine that investment.  
Our Request 
We ask the Building and Planning Department of San Mateo and The Carey School 
administration to reconsider the proposed expansion plans.  We urge the authorities to 
explore alternative solutions that prioritize the needs and concerns of current residents, 
including: 
●​ Implementing more comprehensive traffic studies and ensuring safer 
pedestrian pathways and emergency vehicle access. 
●​ Seeking ways to minimize environmental and neighborhood impact if 
construction occurs. 
●​ Exploring other options for The Carey School expansion for a preschool 
childcare center at some of their other property assets. 
We believe that with thoughtful planning, both the school and the community can thrive 
without any further expansion plans for The Carey School.

San Mateo Planning Commission / City Council 
City of San Mateo 
330 W. 20th Avenue 
San Mateo, CA 94403 
Re: Public Comment – Carey School Early Childhood Center (2033 La Salle Drive) 
Dear Commissioners / Councilmembers, 
As a concerned neighbor/resident, I appreciate the importance of expanding early childhood 
services in San Mateo. However, I respectfully request that the Planning Commission and City 
Council delay approving the proposed development at 2033 La Salle Drive until the applicant 
submits a revised traffic, parking, and safety analysis that reflects real-world conditions and 
addresses key deficiencies in the current documentation. 
Key Traffic & Neighborhood Concerns 
1. Incomplete and Possibly Outdated Traffic Data 
o While the project has submitted a Traffic and Parking Evaluation 
Memorandum prepared by Kimley Horn dated July 22, 2025 City of San 
Mateo, it is unclear whether the traffic counts reflect current neighborhood 
volumes during school hours or considers that a 60% car share rate is going to be 
maintainable or enforceable (notice current rate is only 50% with 40 students).  
Google has spent millions of dollars trying to achieve a 60% car share rate at their 
facilities only to find that they are unattainable. There is no detailed plan in place 
from TCS for maintaining this ratio.   
2. Peak-Hour Congestion Not Adequately Addressed 
o The memo references a “staggered drop-off/pick-up schedule,” but there is no 
detailed intersection turning movement analysis during critical AM/PM peak 
periods. Without this, it's impossible to assess congestion on La Salle Drive or 
adjacent routes. 
3. Potential Parking Spillover 
o Even with parking agreements in place City of San Mateo, the increase in students 
(up to 75 preschoolers) and staff is likely to overload available parking, pushing 
vehicles into nearby residential zones. Assumptions were made when calculating 
the parking stipulation including “specific tenant occupancy percentage” instead 
of a 100% occupancy rate. I believe this is a manipulation of the data that is 
unjustified if/when occupancy rates hit 100% for the 1900-2000 Alameda de las 
Pulgas complex.

--- page 201 ---
4. Safety for Children and Pedestrians Overlooked 
o There is no mention of pedestrian or bicycle safety improvements such as 
crosswalk enhancements, traffic calming, or signage—critical given the proximity 
to Serra High School and residential streets.

What I Urge the City to Require 
For transparency and community protection, I respectfully ask that the application be updated to 
include: 
1. A comprehensive traffic study with current peak-hour data, intersection LOS analysis, 
and cumulative impacts.  
2. Enforceable mitigation measures, including physical safety improvements (like 
crosswalks, signage) and a robust parking management plan. 
3. A monitoring plan with defined responsibilities, timeframes, and enforcement 
mechanisms to respond to unanticipated issues pre-opening and post-opening. Including 
but not limited to decreases in the percentages of ride share participants.  
Approving the project without this updated analysis risks neighborhood safety, traffic 
congestion, and residents' quality of life. I appreciate the City’s commitment to balancing 
community needs with development, and look forward to a solution that supports childcare 
access and safe, livable neighborhoods. 
Thank you for considering these comments.

Patrick Dolan

Patrick Dolan 
9/2/2025 
To: 
City of San Mateo Planning Department 
330 W. 20th Avenue 
San Mateo, CA 94403 
Re: Objection to CEQA Exemption for The Carey School Early Childhood Center Project 
(2033 La Salle Drive) 
Dear Planning Department, 
I am writing to object to the use of a CEQA categorical exemption for The Carey School Early 
Childhood Center project at 2033 La Salle Drive. The applicant’s CEQA Exemption Memo 
dated July 14, 2025, relies on Class 32 (Infill Development) and Class 3 (Small Structures). 
However, neither exemption is appropriate for this institutional project, and several exceptions to 
categorical exemptions apply. 
 
1. Institutional Use Does Not Fit Claimed Exemptions 
The proposed early childhood center is an institutional land use in a single-family (R1-B) 
residential zone, requiring a Special Use Permit. CEQA’s Class 32 exemption is designed for 
residential and commercial infill, not institutional facilities. Likewise, Class 3 is intended for 
“stores, offices, or similar structures” up to 10,000 sq ft — not schools. Attempting to analogize 
a preschool to a “store or office” is a misapplication of the exemption and conflicts with the rule 
that categorical exemptions must be construed narrowly (CEQA Guidelines §15061(b)(2)). 
 
2. Unusual Circumstances Exception (§15300.2(c)) 
Even if a categorical exemption were arguably applicable, the unusual circumstances exception 
invalidates it. 
• 
The project introduces a new institutional facility for 75 students in the middle of a 
single-family neighborhood, separated from the Carey School campus by a creek. 
• 
This is not a typical use in the surrounding R1 zone and constitutes an unusual 
circumstance.

--- page 205 ---
• 
Under Berkeley Hillside Preservation v. City of Berkeley (2015) 60 Cal.4th 1086, an 
exemption cannot be used where unusual circumstances create a reasonable possibility of 
significant impacts. 
Here, traffic congestion, parking overflow, and neighborhood incompatibility present precisely 
that possibility. 
 
3. Traffic and Parking Impacts 
The memo dismisses traffic impacts under C/CAG’s “small project” screening (<110 trips/day). 
This is inadequate because: 
• 
Childcare facilities generate intense, peak-hour trip patterns with clustered drop-offs 
and pick-ups, unlike the dispersed traffic from offices or stores. 
• 
This creates localized congestion and safety hazards that are not captured by daily trip 
counts. 
• 
CEQA requires consideration of the project’s actual operating characteristics, not 
reliance on generic thresholds. 
Given the narrow neighborhood streets and proximity to Serra High School, there is a reasonable 
possibility of significant traffic and safety impacts. 
 
4. Creekside and Tree Impacts 
The site is adjacent to a creek and contains mature trees. The memo downplays these resources, 
yet: 
• 
CEQA prohibits exemptions where scenic or biological resources may be affected 
(§15300.2(d)). 
• 
If protected or heritage trees are removed, or if runoff and impervious surface expansion 
affect creek conditions, the exemption cannot apply. 
• 
A Riparian Setback Assessment alone does not substitute for a full CEQA analysis of 
hydrology, water quality, and tree canopy impacts. 
 
5. General Plan and Zoning Consistency 
Class 32 requires strict consistency with the General Plan and zoning. The General Plan 
designates the site as Single Family, and a school use is only allowed with a Special Use 
Permit. This discretionary overlay demonstrates inconsistency with the base designation, further 
undermining Class 32 eligibility.

--- page 206 ---
Conclusion 
The Carey School project does not qualify for a CEQA exemption. It is an institutional 
development in a residential neighborhood, and its potential impacts — traffic, parking, creek 
adjacency, and tree removal — create unusual circumstances requiring further review. 
At a minimum, the City should prepare an Initial Study to evaluate these issues and determine 
whether a Mitigated Negative Declaration or Environmental Impact Report is required. 
Proceeding by exemption would be legally vulnerable and inconsistent with CEQA’s 
requirements. 
Thank you for your consideration of these comments. 
Sincerely, 
Patrick Dolan 
 Co-Signing: 
Helen Alfassa 
Karen Schuman 
Peggy Karp

Dear Ms. Zakavand, 
 
 
Thank you again for your guidance on the review process for The Carey School’s proposed project at 
2033 La Salle Drive.  I am writing to provide additional comments after reviewing the school’s application 
materials, and to reiterate my strong opposition to The Carey School’s proposed project at 2033 La Salle 
Drive, particularly in light of the claims made in their application. 
 
The school insists that this project will address “neighborhood childcare needs.” That is simply not true. 
My family, along with many others who live in the immediate neighborhood, have applied to Carey 
multiple times over the years and not a single one of our children has been admitted as far as I know. 
Meanwhile, families from Burlingame and Hillsborough are routinely accepted. This pattern makes it 
abundantly clear that the school does not serve our local community’s children, and it is misleading for 
them to suggest otherwise. 
 
For an institution that calls itself a nonprofit, Carey’s actions demonstrate a very different set of 
priorities. Rather than focusing on serving San Mateo families, they appear to favor applicants from more 
affluent neighboring cities. This raises serious questions about their credibility and motives. Moreover, 
the school’s purchase of another residential property at 494 22nd Avenue a few years ago further 
suggests that they are acting more like a real estate investor than an educational institution dedicated to 
the surrounding community. 
 
Approving this project would mean eliminating a residential lot in a neighborhood already struggling with 
housing shortages and replacing it with a facility that does not serve the local population as claimed. 
Instead, it will add more traffic, more noise, and more disruption—while providing no real benefit to the 
families who actually live here. 
 
I urge the Planning Commission and City Council to look past the school’s polished application language 
and weigh their actions and track record. Carey’s proposal does not align with the City’s stated goals of 
equitable access to childcare or preserving neighborhood character. It should not be approved. 
 
Thank you for ensuring these comments are included in the staff report and for holding the school 
accountable to the community it claims to serve. 
 
Sincerely, 
 
A Concerned Neighbor

July 11, 2025

City of San Mateo

City Councilmembers
Planning Commissioners
330 W. 20th Avenue
San Mateo, CA 94403

Subject: The Carey School Early Childhood Center, 2033 La Salle Drive, San Mateo, CA
Dear Members of the San Mateo Planning Commission and City Council,

We, the undersigned, respectfully submit this petition in support of the proposed preschool
located at 2033 La Salle Drive, in a single-family neighborhood in the City of San Mateo. This
project addresses a critical and well-documented need for quality early childhood education and
care within our community.

Addressing Community Need:

San Mateo and the broader County of San Mateo face a significant shortage of childcare
spaces. Needs assessments consistently demonstrate that demand for quality early education
far exceeds the available supply. This shortage leaves working families with limited options,
often resulting in long waitlists and childcare arrangements that do not meet the developmental
needs of our youngest residents. The proposed preschool seeks to alleviate part of this burden
by providing a nurturing, local option for families, including subsidized care for up to 15% of the
spaces.

Alignment with City Policies:

The City of San Mateo’s General Plan outlines clear policies and goals supporting the

expansion of childcare services within neighborhoods. Promoting !ocal childcare not only aligns
with these policies but also strengthens the community by supporting working families, fostering
early childhood development, and contributing to the overall well-being of San Mateo residents.

Balancing Neighborhood Concerns:

We acknowledge the concerns raised by neighboring homeowners, primarily regarding traffic
and noise. In response, this summer we will be conducting a voluntary traffic study and noise
study to evaluate potential impacts. Furthermore, proactive measures will be implemented to
minimize disruptions, including staggered drop-off and pick-up schedules, designated parking
solutions, and sound-mitigating landscaping and building orientation. We will also have school
staff monitoring 20th and La Salle at drop off and pick up. We believe these measures will
address the valid concerns of residents while allowing the community to benefit from
much-needed childcare services.

‘Conclusion: _

We urge the City to recognize the importance of balancing neighborhood concerns with the
critical need for accessible, quality childcare as outlined in its own General Plan. The proposed
preschool represents a thoughtful, community-focused solution to a documented problem, with
mitigation strategies in place to minimize local impacts.

We respectfully ask for your support of this project to help bridge the childcare gap in San Mateo
and provide families with the resources they need to thrive.

HI Simin, 
 
Before I put together a formal response to the new information I want to make sure I understand the 
assumptions being made here and also understand that i am capturing all the new information: 
 
1) The new information involves only the traffic study. Please note the new submission also include a 
parking plan. https://www.cityofsanmateo.org/DocumentCenter/View/99509/The-Carey-Schools-
Parking-and-Traffic-Plan 
 
2) There is no other new  information I may have missed (if there is I would greatly appreciate being 
pointed in the right direction)  
 
3) The current updated report concludes : 
    "The proposed project will generate a net new 133 daily trips, 28 net new trips in the AM peak hour, 19 
net new trips in the School PM peak hour, and 19 net           new trips in the PM peak hour."  pg 15 from 
Kimley Horn.   
     Seems to be a confusing statement since it repeats 19 net new trips in the PM peak hour. (I am 
assuming this is a typo) Yes, those were typos—both should be “27.” These have been corrected in the 
latest version of the report. 
 
4) In the added information it assumes 15 additional vehicles during peak periods on page 12.  The 15 
represents the max number of vehicles (within an hour) expected to use the drop-off or pick-up lane. For

--- page 223 ---
2
the morning, it is the number of vehicles arriving and for the afternoon it is the number of vehicles 
leaving. This is taken directly from the trip generation table. 
 
4) The assumptions of a 60% rideshare assumption remains in place even though there is no mechanism 
to enforce or monitor this. The average rideshare for the past 6 years is 60% but the analysis uses a more 
conservative approach which is based on the lowest rideshare data (50%).  
 
I am a pretty simple man so any help you can give me would be greatly appreciated.   
 
[As a side note I am not sure how the planning department feels about this analysis but if the change to 
the traffic (in the real world) would be 15 vehicles during peak periods  for a school housing 75 students  I 
probably would not be even writing to you.   I may be off here but this analysis does not seem to pass the 
"common sense" test.] 
 
Thank you for all your help to date in this matter and I hope I am not being too much of a burden to you . 
 
Respectfully 
 
Patrick Dolan

From:
Patrick Dolan <
Sent:
Thursday, November 20, 2025 9:36 AM
To:
Simin Zakavand
Subject:
Carey School Expansion -2033 La Salle Drive public comment
Dear Members of the Planning Commission, 
I am writing to respectfully request that the Commission reconsider the assumptions underlying 
approval of The Carey School’s proposed expansion in San Mateo, particularly the conclusion that the 
project “serves the local community” and that the 50% rideshare requirement provides adequate 
mitigation for increased traffic impacts. 
Under the San Mateo Municipal Code, the Planning Commission must base its findings on substantial 
evidence in the record when approving a project. For example, San Mateo Municipal Code §27.08.050(A) 
requires that conditional and special approvals demonstrate, with factual support, that the project is 
“compatible with the surrounding area,” “will not adversely affect the public welfare,” and is “supported 
by evidence in the administrative record.” Findings that are based on assumptions rather than evidence 
do not satisfy these legal requirements. 
Based on the school’s own published materials, Carey draws families from a wide area across the 
Peninsula and even into San Francisco. This means the assumption that the expanded enrollment 
primarily benefits families within the immediate neighborhood is not supported by evidence. An increase 
in student population, when the majority of new families may not live within walking or biking distance of 
the school, does not necessarily equate to serving the surrounding community or “minimizing 
detrimental impacts” as required under SMMC §27.08.050(D). 
Furthermore, the transportation analysis and mitigation plan rely heavily on the assumption that a 50% 
rideshare (carpool) participation rate is realistic and attainable. Under CEQA, mitigation measures must 
be feasible, enforceable, and supported by substantial evidence in the administrative record. CEQA 
Guidelines §§15091 and 15126.4 require that mitigation not be speculative or theoretical. Before this 
assumption can be relied upon to justify approval, the City and applicant should be required to provide: 
• Aggregated, anonymized ZIP code data showing where currently enrolled students reside; 
• Evidence that the majority of new students are likely to live within reasonable distance of the campus 
and therefore realistically able to carpool or use alternative modes of transportation; and 
• A clear mechanism for monitoring, reporting, and enforcement, including consequences for non-
compliance, as required under CEQA Guidelines §15126.4(a)(2). 
Without this information, the rideshare mitigation measure may be speculative and unenforceable. A 
mitigation program that cannot be realistically achieved or enforced does not satisfy CEQA, nor does it 
allow the Planning Commission to make required findings under SMMC §27.08.050. 
An enrollment increase is a substantial intensification of use, and impacts such as increased vehicle 
trips, traffic circulation, queuing, on-street congestion, and neighborhood quality of life must be

--- page 226 ---
2
evaluated using empirical data rather than assumptions. CEQA and the City’s zoning code require that 
such determinations be supported by verifiable evidence. 
I respectfully ask the Planning Commission to require that the applicant provide data demonstrating that 
its transportation and enrollment assumptions are realistic and supported before final approval is 
granted. Doing so ensures that findings of “local benefit” and “adequate mitigation” are legally 
defensible, consistent with CEQA, and consistent with the evidentiary standards of the San Mateo 
Municipal Code. 
Thank you for your consideration. 
Sincerely, 
Patrick Dolan

Re: Support for The Carey School Early Childhood Center 
 
Dear Honorable Mayor and City Councilmembers / Planning Commission Chair and Commissioners -  
On behalf of First 5 San Mateo County, I am pleased to express our strong support for The Carey 
School’s proposed Early Childhood Center in the City of San Mateo. 
 
San Mateo County continues to face a significant shortage of licensed early care and education (ECE) 
spaces, particularly for infants, toddlers, and preschool-aged children. This shortage affects families’ 
ability to work, children’s ability to access high-quality early learning environments, and the stability of our 
local economy. First 5 San Mateo County has long prioritized increasing high-quality ECE capacity, 
improving access and affordability, and supporting the workforce that makes this essential care possible. 
The Carey School’s proposal advances several of these key priorities: 
 
• 
Creates 75 new high-quality spaces for children ages 2–5 at a time when additional capacity is 
critically needed. 
• 
Offers full-day programming (7:45 AM–6:00 PM) that meets the needs of working families 
across the county. 
• 
Commits at least 10% of seats to be fully subsidized, expanding equitable access for families 
who would otherwise face barriers to high-quality care. 
• 
Invests in a modern, purpose-built facility that supports early learning, family engagement, and 
smooth transitions into kindergarten. 
 
These elements reflect the kind of thoughtful, community-responsive expansion that strengthens San 
Mateo County’s early childhood system and aligns with the shared goals outlined in the County’s Early 
Learning and Care Infrastructure efforts. 
 
We appreciate The Carey School’s commitment to expanding access to early care and education and its 
willingness to partner with the community to address one of San Mateo County’s most pressing needs. 
We respectfully encourage the City to approve this proposal. 
 
Thank you for your leadership and for your ongoing support of young children and families in San Mateo. 
 
 
Warm regards, 
 
 
 
 
Kitty Lopez 
Executive Director 
 
cc: Payroll Clerk

To: Planning Commission <PlanningCommission@cityofsanmateo.org>; City Council (San Mateo) 
<CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Support for The Carey School Early Childhood Center 
 
Dear San Mateo City Councilmembers and Planning Commissioners, 
I am writing to you today as a current San Mateo resident and a parent of a 4 year old to express my 
strong support for The Carey School’s proposed Early Childhood Center. 
My wife and I recently went through the daunting process of searching for childcare in San Mateo. As a 
family with two full-time working parents, we experienced firsthand just how difficult it is to find high-
quality care that actually aligns with a full-time work schedule. We found that waitlists are incredibly long 
and that many wonderful programs simply do not offer the extended hours that working families like ours 
desperately need. 
This is why the Carey School’s proposed Early Childhood Center project is so vital for our community. 
The proposed center would add 75 much-needed spaces for children ages 2–5 and, crucially, offer full-
day programming from 7:45 AM to 6:00 PM. This schedule is a game-changer for parents who need to 
work without the constant stress of midday pickups or finding gap coverage. 
By approving this project, you would be directly supporting families who want to stay and work in San 
Mateo but are struggling to find the infrastructure to support them. Expanding high-quality, full-day 
options is one of the most effective ways to support our local workforce and ensuring our community 
remains a viable place for young families to put down roots. 
I strongly urge you to support this project to help address this critical gap in our city’s child care 
resources. 
Thank you for your time and service.

--- page 233 ---
2
Sincerely, 
Dezhi Kang, a San Mateo resident

Dear San Mateo City Councilmembers and Planning Commissioners,

children, currently at different schools - it’s been such a nightmare to find quality care we can
trust and on top of that logistics is not an easy feat.

This is why we strongly support The Carey School’s proposed Early Childhood Center.

We are counting on The Carey School and its Early Childhood Center for reliable and consistent
care for both our children.

This project would add 75 much-needed child-care spaces for children ages 2-5, with full-day
programming from 7:45 AM to 6:00 PM, exactly the kind of schedule that allows parents to work
without constantly worrying about pickup and coverage with a consolidated drop off and pick up
with the elementary school. As a child that heavily benefited from financial aid myself, I’m also
grateful that the project includes some fully subsidized spots to ensure that more families can
access high-quality early education.

We believe early learning in a neighborhood environment is critical to child development and
community building. We also live next to a school and experience first-hand the traffic
congestion during drop off and pick ups - we understand the stress this might put on some
neighbors. However, we know this is a small price to pay for what the neighborhood school
provides for our children and youth. If we cannot have reliable, great schools right in our
neighborhoods, where would we go? Wouldn’t more driving mean more congestion for our city?

| hope you will support this project and help address one of the most pressing needs facing
families in our city.

Thank you very much for your service and consideration.

Hi Danielle,  
 
Thank you for your quick response. We only provide live streaming through YouTube, which does not 
offer LiveChat or participation capabilities. For residents who wish to provide live public comment, 
participation must occur through Zoom. 
 
Here is the link to access our agendas and meeting information: 
https://www.cityofsanmateo.org/3971/Agendas-Minutes-Public-Meeting-Portal 
 
When you open the agenda, you will find the Zoom link and full instructions for providing public 
comment: 
 
During the meeting 
 
By computer: Click the Zoom link at the top of the agenda and you will be added to the meeting. All 
attendees are muted by default. When the item of interest is open for comment, select the “Raise Hand” 
icon and you will be called on at the appropriate time. 
By telephone: Call (408) 638-0968 and enter the conference ID listed at the top of the agenda. When the 
item of interest is open for consideration, press *9 to raise your hand. When called upon, press *6 to 
unmute, state your name, and provide your comments. 
By Zoom (app or browser): Click the Zoom link at the top of the agenda and you will be added to the 
meeting. All attendees are muted by default. When the item of interest is open for consideration, select 
the “Raise Hand” icon and you will be called on to speak. 
In person: At the meeting, complete a “Request to Speak” form.
